###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: conversion from Microfocus NetExpress COBOL to Fujitsu netCOBOL.net

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Robert Wolfe <rtwolfe78@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-24T14:33:02-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b7e85c5e-dc23-42bb-9a0f-90a48758b951@gy7g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1826132.525.1318451603192.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbau1> <7411645a-c787-437b-8840-3b3b65209774@ff5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <kS5pq.2$zC6.0@en-nntp-15.dc1.easynews.com> <9gkd1tFudeU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete,

You wrote:

"I can see no point to converting between Micro Focus and Fujitsu
implementations of COBOL. Both provide support for .Net COBOL and both
have
excellent products.

Why would you do it?

I have to believe that this kind of exercise is politically and/or
marketing
motivated."

It's not politically motivated, it's almost always economically
motivated.  We support compiler independence, therefore "we have no
dog in the fight" when it comes to the choice of a particular
compiler.......from our perspective, it truly is whatever the customer
wants it to be.

There are still, lots of companies who want to continue to enhance and
maintain their COBOL application using COBOL as the primary language.
I know that you suggest otherwise, but we do speak to companies daily
who have no intention of moving away from COBOL.  That's why they call
us.

We talk to companies that prefer COBOL to other languages because
their staff is productive and proficient in COBOL and it costs a
significant amount of money to retrain large groups of programmers.

The reason companies are migrating to Fujitsu COBOL is because they do
not charge runtime distribution fees and that can result in a
considerable decrease in application deployment costs.

From our perspective, it is purely economics, not politics or
marketing.

[snip]
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: conversion from Microfocus NetExpress COBOL to Fujitsu netCOBOL.net

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-10-25T12:59:59+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gmcg1Fc37U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1826132.525.1318451603192.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbau1> <7411645a-c787-437b-8840-3b3b65209774@ff5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <kS5pq.2$zC6.0@en-nntp-15.dc1.easynews.com> <9gkd1tFudeU1@mid.individual.net> <b7e85c5e-dc23-42bb-9a0f-90a48758b951@gy7g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Robert Wolfe wrote:
> Pete,
>
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> [snip]

A fair response, Bob.

I guess the run time fees are a big consideration for many people.

That one has been discussed here at length over years :-)

I don't doubt what you say (if I was committed to COBOL I would probably be 
talking to Flexus too) :-)

Nevertheless, the majority of sites are trying to move off DEPENDENCY on 
COBOL and in that arena run time fees don't mean much because you can get 
the compiler, IDE, AND distribution rights for FREE, if you DON'T use COBOL.

It's a hard offer to beat.

However, there is re-training involved and some companies simply won't spend 
money on training.

Others just haven't realised that the game has changed. The realization is 
gradually being pushed onto them as the existing crop of COBOL people retire 
or die off.

Nevertheless, I'd be the frst to admit that there is still a valuable 
investment in COBOL which many companies won't want to write off. (Of course 
many other companies are glad to be shot of it... :-))

Would you be prepared to commit your company to a platform migration ONLY to 
avoid runtime fees?

I wouldn't.

I'd want much more value for that amount of aggravation.:-)

It's time for people to consider where they are and where they are going. If 
you are going to do a platform migration, it might as well be in a direction 
that moves away from COBOL altogether, or at least gives you the option to 
do so.

If you can leverage your existing code into a new environment, WITHOUT 
remaining dependent on COBOL (as well as getting rid of your run time fees), 
that looks more attractive to me than simply swapping the status quo because 
of run time fees.

Our clients are doing new development in C# and VB.Net  (some are still 
using COBOL...) while still retaining the essential core of their COBOL 
legacy and running it in the new environment as OO code. They have an 
OPTION. Not locked in to COBOL, but able to continue using it if they want 
to.

That is the area which PRIMA is seeking to fill.

Pete.
```

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Tue, 25 Oct 2011 12:59:59 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[98 more quoted lines elided]…
> businesses and other organizations the choices aren't as simple.

Yes, I should have stated that we are committed to the Microsoft environment 
and so are our clients.

I have deliberately refrained from moving to mainframe migration because 
there would need to be some heavy investment for us before we could support 
it properly. (I also don't think I want to compete directly with Alchemy and 
Micro Focus, who have resources I can only dream of...:-))

I have run preliminary tests with creation of RDB and VSAM/KSDS being 
converted to RDB and it was very successful, but we generate an OO Data 
Access Layer at the same time and that would need to be changed for 
mainframe. The layer could be generated as called modules or it could be 
generated in Java (for example), or it could even be implemented in SQL as 
stored procedures on the actual database, but to do all of that would take 
time and money.

At the moment I'm more interested in making sure we can get people to Win 8 
and Metro without too much sweat... :-)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Difference

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>
- **Date:** 2011-10-19T23:48:34-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6cmdnWtueZ27NQLTnZ2dnUVZ5h2dnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<c566768d-65e3-4e4a-9e18-0fda6357e1db@p14g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c566768d-65e3-4e4a-9e18-0fda6357e1db@p14g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 10/19/2011 7:52 AM, Sheky wrote:
> what is the difference between 999v12 and 999.12?
>
> And what is significance of 77? how is it different from 01 ?
>

I don't believe 999v12 is a legal picture clause.  Did you perhaps mean 
999V99?

A level 77 data item in COBOL is always an elementary item.  In some 
early COBOL compilers a level 77 item might have been allocated to a 
different type of memory.

A level 01 data item can be an elementary item but is usually a group 
item.  It can be subdivided into lower lever group and/or elementary items.

77  77-DATE            PIC X(08) VALUE SPACES.

01  WS-EDITED-DATE.
     05  WS-YEAR        PIC X(04) VALUE SPACES.
     05  FILLER         PIC X(01) VALUE '-'.
     05  WS-MONTH       PIC X(02) VALUE SPACES.
     05  FILLER         PIC X(01) VALUE '-'.
     05  WS-DAY         PIC X(02) VALUE SPACES.

In the example above, 77-DATE, WS-YEAR, WS-MONTH, and WS-DAY are all 
named elementary data items.  They are not further subdivided. 
WS-EDITED-DATE is a group item, containing five elementary items.
```

#### ↳ Re: Difference

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-21T09:26:21-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<moadnet2w5yNHDzTnZ2dnUVZ_gWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<c566768d-65e3-4e4a-9e18-0fda6357e1db@p14g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
Sheky wrote:
> what is the difference between 999v12 and 999.12?
>
> And what is significance of 77? how is it different from 01 ?

First, "v12" and ".12" are illegal.

If you are asking about the difference between a "v" and a "period," the 
difference is:

"v" asserts the variable is used internally for computation.

"period" is actually a decimal point and is used when the variable is to be 
viewed by a human.

In general, "v" variables look like crap when you try to print or view them 
and "decimal point" variables cannot be used in computations.

Use one or the other depending on the sentence.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Currying the code

- **From:** Jessica Colman <jessica.colman@augustakom.net>
- **Date:** 2011-10-27T00:36:00+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<j8a20h$us9$1@news.m-online.net>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9gp6usFfo6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9gc73fFiqgU1@mid.individual.net> <j7sele$nfb$1@news.m-online.net> <9gp6usFfo6U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Usually Maven is used for Java builds. The key thing is the "convention 
over configuration" aspect. That is, as long as your project follows a 
certain layout, you can use a very simple default pom.xml for building, 
reporting and documentation. E.g. all your Java code has to be in the 
"src/main/java" directory, all tests must be in "src/test/java" etc.

This simplifys the build dramatically. Whereas every ANT build script is 
sligthly different, a pom.xml may be reused for a lot of builds.

The aspect I do not like is (and that is the reason for my posting), 
that Maven includes every library, which _may_ be nescessary. If. e.g. 
you need the org.apache.commons.lang.StringUtils class, you end up with 
an application including almost every org.apache.commons-library 
(commons.net, commons.logging, commons.io, etc). In the end, your 
application is twice as big as nescessary, including libraries you never 
heard of and you have no idea, if you really need them or not.

Nevertheless Maven is extremely powerfull and, as long as you project 
follows Maven's layout, easy to use. I know that IBM has a ANT/z for 
COBOL and PL/I builds so maybe a Maven/z is not far away.

Jessica
```

```
Not much further: In a web application, your java classes go to 
WEB-INF/classes. All dependant libraries (i.e. jars) go to WEB-INF/lib. 
Using Maven, you end up with a WEB-INF/lib directory, containing dozens 
of jar files, although you really need a couple one of them.

Greetings

Jessica
```

```
   wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com

      * $SET Dialect(ANS85) FLAGAS(W) AREACHECK
       Identification Division.
        PROGRAM-ID. SIVSXX.
       DATA DIVISION.
        WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
         1 A PIC XX VALUE IS "1 ".
         1 B PIC XX VALUE IS "10".
         1 I PIC 9P VALUE IS 10.
       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
        Mainline.
           IF I EQUAL A
             DISPLAY "This implementation is standard-compliant."
      *       MOVE 1 TO RETURN-CODE
                .
           IF I EQUAL B
             DISPLAY "This implementation has the alternative "
                 "interpretation."
      *       MOVE 2 TO RETURN-CODE
                 .
           IF I NOT EQUAL A AND I NOT EQUAL B
             DISPLAY "This implementation has yet another "
                 "interpretation."
      *       MOVE 3 TO RETURN-CODE
                .
           STOP RUN.
```

## Thread: 77 Levels — 26 this month / 161 total

### 77 Levels

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-23T21:59:09-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
There was a recent thread with a question abou 77-levels. The quesiton 
appeared to be from a "do my homework" person, but I have my own quesiton 
abut this.  (and this is NOT homework <G>)

At one time, I heard that SOME compilers actually did "different things" 
with 77-levels versus an 01-level with no subordiant items (i.e. an 
elmentary 01-level).  I think at least one used different "word boundaries" 
for 77-levels and another placed 77-level entries either in a different type 
of storage or all "contiguous" at the beginning of storage (regardless ow 
where they were defined in worksint-storage).

So my quesiton is,

  Does anyone know of any "currently supported" compilers that do anything 
different with a 77-level from an elementary 01-leve?  If so, what is the 
difference (and what is the compiler)?

My *guess* is that none of the currently supported compilers make any 
difference (at least by default), but this is only a guess.
```

#### ↳ Re: 77 Levels

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-10-24T18:05:39+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gka15F93qU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> There was a recent thread with a question abou 77-levels. The quesiton
> appeared to be from a "do my homework" person, but I have my own
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> difference (at least by default), but this is only a guess.

The major difference (40 years ago) was the alignment of  items in storage. 
In the days when storage was expensive and in short supply, you could save 
bytes by usng 77 levels because they were allocated directly into storage 
without regard for any kind of boundary.

Different acrhitectures could perform better if they didn't have to modify 
addresses to align storage and compilers could save storage if they didn't 
have to allocate "slack" bytes to force internal alignment  (to a "word 
boundary") of stored items.

But of all of this has been irrelevant for decades now.

These days the use of a 77 level is just plain pointless and unnecessary. It 
has NO advantage over a 77 level, and it simply confuses people having to 
deal with COBOL legacy.

The ONLY use I would see for it would be for compatibility with ancient 
code.

Even if there was some esoteric COBOL compiler, running on some weird 
hardware that no-one's ever heard of, where boundary alignment actually 
improves performance, the AMOUNT of performance improvement is likely to be 
so slight that it would make no discernible difference.

I'm sticking with 01 levels...

Pete.

Use 01 and
```

```
In our mainframe application programs, we do make use of 77 levels
though not to the extent that we used to in days gone by.  The trend
now is to have maybe a couple of flags as 77 levels but to define most
other fields under various 01 level identifiers.  Like 01
WS-Working-Fields.  Then 05 or whatever you wish (our standard is 05)
fields under it usually prefixed by the 01 name.

For our clients that use Panvalet as a source library management, it
will add a 77 level item to all COBOL programs indicating module name,
level number, compile date and time.

But as I said, we do not utilize 77 levels as much as we used to.
```

```
> At one time, I heard that SOME compilers actually did "different things" 
> with 77-levels versus an 01-level with no subordiant items (i.e. an 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> (regardless ow where they were defined in worksint-storage).

An 01 was always defined by IBM to be on a doubleword boundary. I believe
this is still the case but I don't have a z/OS system to test. 77 items were
aligned based on the data item defined.

>   Does anyone know of any "currently supported" compilers that do anything> different with a 77-level from an elementary 01-leve?  If so, what is the 
> difference (and what is the compiler)?

I would bet IBM has not changed this behavior and it is still relevant,
especially when processing tapes or when accessing storage in the LINKAGE
SECTION.

I disagree with what Pete says if I understand him to mean 77 should not be
used. Not every item is part of a structure or record, this should be very
obvious. There are elementary data items and they should be defined as
77. If that's not what you meant Pete then apologies.
```

```
> On 10/24/2011 6:30 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Mattc wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> absolutely necessary in those cases.

Not only unnecessary, but pointless. The compiler allocates the maximum 
storage anyway; it isn't dynamic storage as in other languages.

There MAY be a case that it can shorten the time taken for table entry 
compare. But by the time it has worked out the length and gone to registers 
a storage based compare would have succeeded anyway... (Unless the length of 
each table entry is VERY long... for normal purposes there is no real gain.)

I don't avoid it because I don't understand it; I avoid it because I DO 
understand it :-)

> programs.

Yes, of course.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <9gmaolFmhU1@mid.individual.net> <JZ6dnVTtlcf_-jvTnZ2dnUVZ5hOdnZ2d@giganews.com> <9gnnk1Ff54U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Oct 26, 1:15 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Arnold Trembley wrote:
> > On 10/24/2011 6:30 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> storage anyway; it isn't dynamic storage as in other languages.

Dynamic storage can be very expensive in processing time. In general
there is a heap which is a fixed size initially from which storage can
be allocated or freed. When it becomes full or too fragmented another
large block is requested from the OS.

If a dynamic table is often added to or reduced then there may be
frequent expensive calls to alloc(), realloc() and free() or their
equivalents.

While ODO may actually use a fixed size table this has trivial
overheads compared to dynamic tables.

> There MAY be a case that it can shorten the time taken for table entry
> compare. But by the time it has worked out the length and gone to registers
> a storage based compare would have succeeded anyway... (Unless the length of
> each table entry is VERY long... for normal purposes there is no real gain.)

Is this just more uninformed dogma ?

> I don't avoid it because I don't understand it; I avoid it because I DO
> understand it :-)

You may understand the semantics but you seem to think that the
implementation of dynamic tables is magic.
```

```
between executions (data from external sources, etc.).  If the unused
slots aren't initialized to maximum values interesting results could
occur.  They might also occur if less than half of the maximum number
of possible entries are loaded.
```

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
>
> So assuming you were writing something in COBOL where the number of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I wouldn't.

I'd write the data to a ISAM (or similar) file instead of a table. 
Thereafter, use file access to find what I wanted.

Before you blanch, with modern compilers, and the operating system's 
optimization of memory, I'd be really surprised if my suggestion wasn't 
faster than a binary search in RAM!

This file-method has the following benefits (in addition to speed)
* The executable module is smaller
* You need never worry - as a practical matter - about how large the table 
may get
* You don't have to kludge around COBOL's table size limitations
```

```
> I was always taught to use 77s for single line data items, and to use

01  MISC-THINGS GLOBAL.

  02  BIGGER-THING...

  02  DARNED-THINGS.
      05  DARNED-THING-1...
      05  DARNED-THING-2...
  02  EDIBLE-THING...
  --- etc ---

I know, I know, but I LIKE to alphabetize working storage.
```

<a id="t-f91583a32e"></a>
## Thread: Oddity fixed — 6 msgs

### Oddity fixed

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-25T14:59:23-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gYednfZA_ciGiDrTnZ2dnUVZ_uadnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Most will probably know this fix I discovered yesterday. But for those that 
don't, well. . .

The problem is found in column widths in ListView and other boxes under 
PowerCobol. When the user punches the "full screen" button, the column 
widths get all wonky and do not enlarge proportionally.

Here's the fix.
A. Find the width of the entire form at normal screen mode (say, 10,000)
B. Find the width of each column (say 1000, 2000, and 4000).
C. Compute the ratio/percentage of each column's width compared to the total 
width of the form. (0.10, 0.20, and 0.40)
D. At the form event "Resized" drop in the following code:

   COMPUTE WS-WIDTH = POW-CLIENTWIDTH * 0.10.
   MOVE WS-WIDTH TO 'WIDTH' OF COLUMNS(1) OF ListView-POS.

   COMPUTE WS-WIDTH = POW-CLIENTWIDTH * 0.20.
   MOVE WS-WIDTH TO 'WIDTH' OF COLUMNS(2) OF ListView-POS.

    etc.

My users are so deliriously happy they want to have my babies! (Regrettably, 
by IV fertilization.)

After almost 11 years using this product, I'm still learning/discovering new 
things.
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Oddity fixed

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-26T21:24:14-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<x4ydnXalg6NSXTXTnZ2dnUVZ_uGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<gYednfZA_ciGiDrTnZ2dnUVZ_uadnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9gp4l9FfrU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> HeyBub wrote:
>> Most will probably know this fix I discovered yesterday. But for
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Do you believe I'm insane?

I tried to call them today to beg for technical support even though they 
state on their web site that if you're not using the current version (I'm 
not) AND have paid them enormous sums per year, you can put your lips on an 
exhaust pipe.

Well, their technical support hours are something like 8:30 a.m. until 8:31 
a.m. I got a recording.

My problem was:
a. ALL PowerCobol programs simply refused to start with NO error message.
b. ALL PowerCobol projects refused to open with the highly informative 
message "Could not open xxxx.ppj"

So, while waiting for the clock to reach the appointed minute, and after 
laying out all my credit cards on the desk, steeling myself for the 
anticipated significant reduction in my personal wealth, I decided to do 
some snooping on my own.

I found the problem.

I have a solid-state 60Gig hard drive where access time is measured in 
microseconds. I have my system TEMP file assigned to that drive. Well, guess 
what?

The freakin' drive went south. It was no more. It was singing in the 
heavenly choir - it couldn't be accessed for love or money. I formatted the 
drive (took about eight seconds) and everthing returned to normal!

Meanwhile, I discovered how to actually DO multi-select in a listview box. 
Here's the trick (of course the LV box has to be flagged as multi-select) 
showing how to delete multiple, selected, items:

   MOVE "Count" OF LV-BOX TO WSCOUNT.
   PERFORM VARYING WSINDX FROM 1 BY 1 UNTIL WSINDX > WSCOUNT
    IF 'Selected' OF "ListItems"(WSINDX) OF LV-BOX = POW-TRUE
     INVOKE LV-BOX 'Delete' USING WSINDX
     SUBTRACT 1 FROM WSINDX
     MOVE 'Count' OF LV-BOX TO WSCOUNT
    END-IF
   END-PERFORM.
   IF WSCOUNT > 0
     INVOKE 'ListItems'(1) OF LV-BOX "EnsureVisible"
  END-IF.

The "IF" statement above contains the technique you won't find explicitly in 
any of the manuals, help files, or sample programs.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Oddity fixed

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-10-28T13:05:15+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gu9tsFcpoU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<gYednfZA_ciGiDrTnZ2dnUVZ_uadnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9gp4l9FfrU1@mid.individual.net> <x4ydnXalg6NSXTXTnZ2dnUVZ_uGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> HeyBub wrote:
…[80 more quoted lines elided]…
> explicitly in any of the manuals, help files, or sample programs.

Ah, you have come to the same conclusion I did; don't ask them for help, do 
it yourself. :-) (It is faster, better and definitely much cheaper.)

I haven't personally used PowerCOBOL for quite a while now but many years 
ago I wrote a paper on using the ListView with it (several people had 
written to me saying they were having problems with it). This paper was 
later translated into Spanish and Portuguese (not by me :-)) and I believe 
it is still available on some South American websites.

I'll have a look across some old laptops and see if I can find the original 
article.

As I recall, I tumbled to the fact that the PowerCOBOL ListView control uses 
the POW-NODE structure to populate itself. Rather than addressing the 
ListView directly, I did all the manipulation using the underlying Node 
structure (which is a collection with certain useful functions built into 
it.) Consequently I never had problems with adding, changing, or deleting, 
single or multi selected items.

I realise now (after using C# for a few years) that a decent control has 
separate collections for what is selected and what is not. This just makes 
everything really simple without the need to look at or maintain counts or 
indexes. (You just iterate over the collection you want with a simple 
"foreach")

I still receive a few requests each month for help with PowerCOBOL from 
people who share your frustration with Alchemy's non-support of it. 
(Although their web site shows it as a proud member of their product set, I 
think their focus is elsewhere... ) The fact is that people who are 
committed to PowerCOBOL are probably going down a dead end through no fault 
of their own. I used it for years and was very impressed with it, but I 
wouldn't go back to it after using .Net.

I have a client at the moment whose entire business is based on PowerCOBOL. 
Currently they are moving all their indexed data to SQL Server and have used 
the PRIMA Migration Toolset to do this.  You can imagine what a job it is 
with hundreds of PowerCOBOL projects to go through and Transform every 
scriptlet that uses ISAM, into using invokes of the Data Access Layer. 
Nevertheless the Toolset identifies and strips out the scriptlets, changes 
the code and then places the changed scriptlets into a schedule for pasting 
back in the project. (The whole transformation process for them takes a 
couple of hours.)

I am really annoyed that I can't just update the project files. It is a 
proprietary format and not documented. The PowerCOB program accepts certain 
parameters for building and rebuilding projects, but not for editing. You 
can't call PowerCOB and update a scriptlet or even look at a scriptlet. (As 
far as I can tell; if anybody reading this knows differently PLEASE let us 
know...)

I'm too busy at the moment to investigate this further and we can live with 
pasting the scriptlets back, but I haven't given up on it yet...

PowerCOBOL is a perfect example of good software that got overtaken by 
events and is no longer of interest to the people who sell it.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-b881b8a06a"></a>
## Thread: ODO's, Dynamic Storage, and Standards — 1 this month / 3 total

_Topics: [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards)_

### ODO's, Dynamic Storage, and Standards

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-25T16:21:06-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<yDGpq.3519$rt7.441@en-nntp-05.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
(Follow on to another thread - with subject chagne)

For what it is worth (possibly nothing <G>)

The current draft revion of the COBOL Standard introduces two new features:

 - Dynamic Capacity Tables
 - Dynamic length elementary items

The former actually provides for ODO's where only the "currently needed" 
storage is allocated (and this amount can either grwo or shrink, based on 
explicit or implicit needs.  (These tables may be nested in other dynamic 
capacity or "traditional" tables)

the latter is (sort of) like C null-terminated strings, i.e. it is an 
elementary itme that can either be prefixed or suffixed in such a way to 
specify its current length - again this can change during execution

The specifications in the draft revision have gone thru many variations and 
I think they are now relatively reasonable - from a programmers point of 
view.

HOWEVER, as has been stated in the other thread, from what I have heard 
run-time "cost" of such features many turn out to be prohibitive.  Also, as 
stated elesewhere, the influence of the Standrd is minimal.  Both of these 
make me wonder if either or both of these features will ever make it into 
any generally available COBOL compilers.  Nevertheless, these are features 
that also demonstrate an ATTEMPT in the Standard process to provicde 
"well-defined" fetures that are in response to actual COBOL programmer 
"needs" (well they were needed in the '70s, 80s, etc).
```

---

<a id="t-09c15315ff"></a>
## Thread: Soundex — 13 this month / 14 total

### Soundex

- **From:** James Gavan <jgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2011-10-26T21:23:29-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<M84qq.994$rF5.977@newsfe19.iad>`

```
As a result of reading the topics Soundex and Metaphone, I checked up on 
the topic both historically and in terms of programming. Starts with US 
Census and then I see that some 6 states in the U.S. have applications 
for using names associated with vehicle licensing.

At this point I'm not clear as to its usefulness in the general 
environment We can look at Michael Turk's Soundex program for the basic 
algorithm. Then we have Jerry fine-tuning Soundex to make it more 
effective on different sound groups. As the web literature indicates 
there are also problems with German names, Jewish names originating 
either from German or the Slavic languages, plus problems with Hebrew 
names, and it goes on an on. There's the problem with "British" names :-

Pronounced		Spelling
----------		-------
Affleck			Auchinleck		
Chumlee			Cholmondeley
Fanshaw			Featherstonhaugh	
Taves			Towes

OK, Ben Affleck's family used the pronounced version. Imagine the 
surprised look on the face of the Univac sales manager for Calgary, (in 
'75), when I told him the generally accepted pronunciation of his 
surname in the UK was 'Fanshaw'. And the above  are just the tip of the 
iceberg. (see 
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_names_in_English_with_counterintuitive_pronunciations#C_3).

Those US states using it for vehicles - I wouldn't mind betting there 
are six customized versions, built up over years of experience.

I'm aware that both Oracle and Microsoft have Soundex syntax as part of 
their SQLs - but are their end results any more precise ?

PECD : "I've implemented it (Soundex) with database searches and found 
it extremely useful. Especially for people who don't have English as a 
first language but must do searches on things like English names".

I guess you just got lucky in view of the following comments.

Soundex Accuracy :-

---------------------------------------------------------------------
http://www.immagic.com/eLibrary/ARCHIVES/GENERAL/LAS_US/L030206B.pdf

"Two recent studies looked at the performance of the basic Soundex 
algorithm, using statistical measures to gauge accuracy.

Alan Stanier (September 1990, Computers in Genealogy,
Vol. 3, No. 7) extracted all 411,716 surnames from the 1851 U.S. Census 
sample and linked related name forms based on information provided in a 
dictionary of surnames. Results :-

a) 33% of the matches that would be returned by Soundex would be correct.
b) 25% of correct matches would fail to be discovered by Soundex.

A second study by A. J. Lait and B. Randell (1996) compared the 
performance of several name-matching algorithms, including the basic 
Soundex method.  Searches were conducted on a data set of 5600 unique 
surnames, chosen to represent names beginning with each letter of the 
alphabet at a frequency of occurrence reflecting actual alphabetic
distributions of names, and including as well names of varying lengths.
The study found that Soundex (judged to be the best of the four 
algorithms compared) returned - Results :-

b) 36.37% of the actual correct matches
c) 60% of names that were correct matches for query names were not 
returned...."

----------------------------------------------------------------------

So could both Jerry and Pete illustrate examples of how they are using 
Soundex ? Does somebody say "The guy's name sounds like 'Mack-mood 
Arm-dine-jad', I think". Replies the operator at the keyboard, "Macmood 
WHO ?".

Frankly the Soundex approach looks cute but fiddly and it would appear, 
based on the title type, (surname, product description etc.), that you 
are searching on, it isn't very precise. I'd be foolish to make a 
suggestion at this stage without knowing *exactly* how they are applying 
the Soundex search results. There just may be a simpler alternative to 
searching on title types. Let's see what they have to say first. My idea 
is not restricted to COBOL but in theory at least, should be available 
in any OO language.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
In article <699e86c6-91be-4ae7-b706-a0cfd1406b04@a17g2000yqj.googlegroups.com>,
Alistair Maclean  <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> wrote:
>On Oct 27, 4:23?am, James Gavan <jga...@shaw.ca> wrote:
>> There's the problem with "British" names :-
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> Affleck ? ? ? ? ? ? ? ? Auchinleck ? ? ? ? ? ? ?

[snip]

>Interesting. I have only heard of Auchinleck being pronounced as it is
>spelled. I've never heard of the Affleck variant.

As I read it, Mr Maclean, 'Auchinleck' contains an initial diphthong.  
Diphtong pronunciation can vary within the same language as well as across 
languages.  'Au' might be rendered as 'oh' as well as 'ow'.

>
>And:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Also, does Goldstein get pronounced as Goldstine or Goldsteen?

I've heard both.  Notice that the ambiguity rests in the 'ei' diphthong; 
the long 'i' pronunciation (indicated by your Anglicised '-ine' suffix, 
where the common rule is that a final, unpronounced 'e' gives the 
immediately-preceding vowel a long pronounciation (compare 'ban' with 
'bane', then compare 'bane' with 'bain' (Fr.)) is the in accord with the 
German pronounciation of 'ei' whereas the long e (indicated by the doubled 
vowel) is usually the... Anglicised pronounciation.

I seem to recall George Gershwin writing a ditty about such difficulties.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: Soundex

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-27T17:18:34-05:00

```
James Gavan wrote:
>
> Frankly the Soundex approach looks cute but fiddly and it would
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Without getting off in the weeds over what names sound like what, here's our 
application: A customer approaches the desk and requests that the purchase 
be charged. "And what is your name?" asks the clerk.

"Citizen Kane" says the customer. The clerk begins typing "C - A ..." and 
pauses for two seconds. The screen then fills with (from an actual database 
on my computer):

Michael a Cabrera
John Cadamuro
Ward C. Caddington
Lillian S. Cahill
Timothy Cahill
Dough Cain
Brett Caine
Katheryn Caine
(and about another 200 names followed by)
--- Sounds like ---
Citizen Kane
Richard Kay
Edward Kay
Jeff Kaye
Martin J. Kehoe
Matthew Kehoe
Dr Michael Key
Boo Soon Khoo

If the clerk is overwhelmed by the first 200 names, she can add another 
letter "C-A-I" and the screen responds with mere six names (on my 17,000 
name database) plus the "K" guys as above.

In our rendition, Soundex doesn't attempt to uniquely identify the request, 
it only assists in cutting down the collection to a manageable list.
```

```
An interesting post, Jimmy.

I hadn't seen those statistics before.

The company I used it for was based in London and it was in the early 1980s. 
I built a "database" for them using COBOL and indexed files. I heard about 
soundex being used for the 19th century US census, and was intrigued that 
anybody was doing data processing on a large scale at that time. I thought 
it might be useful so I added it to the database facilities. Later I amended 
the algorithm and it was the later version that I posted here. It is very 
possible that because it was London based this skewed the success rate, or 
it is just possible that nobody worried if they couldn't find a name with 
it; I don't know. (Perhaps anything is better than nothing...:-)) The 
feedback I got from the owner of the business was that the girls she had 
doing searches used it easily and found it very helpful. They were mainly 
Scandinavian and German.  (Perhaps she was just being "polite" to me...) 
Thats really all I know about how useful it was.

I'm surprised by your statistics but I guess 36% is way better than zero :-)

As for giving an example of how I use it today, I can't because I don't. I 
have the component whose code I posted here plugged into one or two 
databases (MS Access, mainly, and I have since migrated to SQL Server as my 
preferred DB) and I will be updating the component with HeyBub's changes, 
but I haven't actually used Soundex for about 25 years.

Pete.
```

## Thread: Call yourselfa Programmer? — 2 this month / 14 total

_Thread started [2011-11](2011-11.md#t-ea07671b0a)._

#### ↳ Re: Call yourselfa Programmer?

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-31T10:50:24-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<u2cta7l7t8gm2o77r7aftaq8i5ej87b2r5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9h7hqgFj9iU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Tue, 1 Nov 2011 01:15:10 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>This is one of the best written blog entries I have read for a long time.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.

Not sure if the author of that blog is or was or ever has been a
programmer and, if he is/was, ever worked in a software house.  I
would dispute his statements which follow::

"There are companies which create software which actually gets used by
customers, which describes almost everything that you probably think
of when you think of software.  It is unlikely that you will work at
one unless you work towards making this happen.  Even if you actually
work at one, many of the programmers there do not work on
customer-facing software, either."

My empolyer, for the most part, is one of those "create software"
companies and except for some underlying utility and online
transaction build type systems, everything we write gets used by our
customers.  We currently offer over 500 applications to 14,000
customers in over 100 countries.  So, even if you are a hired gun like
I am, you will be working on "customer facing" software.  That is true
for 98% of our programmers.  And I would venture to say that is true
for most software houses in any industry.

The author also mentions Peter Drucker and his cost center/profit
center terminology.  My company adopted such a breakdown sometime in
the early 90's.  Now maybe in some places, programmers may be in a
cost center and those in developement probably fall into that
category.  But once something is developed and goes on the market, the
programmers supporting that software are now in a profit center.  The
author says, "Engineers in particular are usually very highly paid
Cost Centers, which sets MBAï¿½s optimization antennae to twitching.
This is what brings us wonderful ideas like outsourcing, which is
ï¿½Letï¿½s replace really expensive Cost Centers who do some magic which
we kinda need but donï¿½t really care about with less expensive Cost
Centers in a lower wage country.  Nobody ever outsources Profit
Centers."

Another ridiculous idea born of probably never having worked at a
software house.  As I stated,  before, once software is developed and
goes on the market, programmers supporting it are part of the profit
center.  I have seen, for a brief time, entire applications' support
outsourced to programmers in India.  Once it was realized that the
quality of work was not as good, the support was moved back to the US
and the Indian company used for more mundane tasks like coding
conversions or installation assistance, or upgrades due to regulation
changes but this is done under the guidance of a US based person.

In short, maybe the blogger had some experiences as an Engineer and
what he writes might be true, I don't know.  I've only worked as a
programmer and am more than happy to identify myself as such.

Regards,
```
